
Source: Efficiently Constructing Sparse Navigable Graphs - NSF Public Access Repository
URL: https://par.nsf.gov/servlets/purl/10644416
Efficiently Constructing Sparse Navigable Graphs∗ Alex Conway† Laxman Dhulipala‡ Martin Farach-Colton§ Rob Johnson¶ Ben Landrum∗ Christopher Musco‡ Yarin Shechter‡ Torsten Suel‡ Richard Wen† Abstract. Graph-based nearest neighbor search methods have seen a surge of popularity in recent years, offering state-of-the-art performance across a wide variety of applications. Central to these methods is the task of constructing a sparse navigable search graph for a given dataset endowed with a distance function. Unfortunately, doing so is computationally expensive, so heuristics are universally used in practice. In this work, we initiate the study of fast algorithms with provable guarantees for search graph construction. For a dataset with n data points, the problem of constructing an optimally sparse navigable graph can be framed as n separate but highly correlated minimum set cover instances. This yields a naive O(n3) time greedy algorithm that returns a navigable graph whose sparsity is at most O(logn) higher than optimal. We improve significantly on this baseline, taking advantage of correlation between the set cover instances to leverage techniques from streaming and sublinear-time set cover algorithms. By also introducing problem-specific pre-processing techniques, we obtain an Õ(n2) time algorithm for constructing an O(logn)-approximate sparsest navigable graph under any distance function. The runtime of our method is optimal up to logarithmic factors under the Strong Exponential Time Hypothesis via a reduction from the Monochromatic Closest Pair problem. Moreover, we prove that, as with general set cover, obtaining better than an O(log n)-approximation is NP-hard, despite the significant additional structure present in the navigable graph problem. Finally, we show that our approach can also beat cubic time for the closely related and practically important problems of constructing α-shortcut reachable and τ -monotonic graphs, which are also used for nearest neighbor search. For such graphs, we show that black-box sublinear set cover algorithms yield Õ(n2.5) time or better algorithms. 1 Introduction Nearest neighbor search has been central in computer science and data science for decades [2, 4, 6, 7, 11, 28, 31, 35, 40]. Current interest in the problem is driven by its central role in modern AI systems: search over machine-learned semantic embeddings is the key technology behind AI-driven web search, retrieval-augmented generation in LLMs, and more [9, 34,41,42,55]. Formally, the nearest neighbor search problem is defined as follows: Given a point set P = {p1, . . . , pn} and a distance function d(·, ·),1 construct a data structure to efficiently find argmini∈{1,...,n} d(pi, q) for any given query point q, or an approximate minimizer. A naive O(n) time method is to brute-force search over the entire dataset, so “efficiently” here means finding the nearest neighbor in o(n) time. The nearest neighbor search problem has been studied extensively both in theory and in practice. A classical solution is locality-sensitive hashing (LSH) [3, 28], which provides theoretical guarantees on the approximation ratio, construction time, and query time. However, LSH has proven too inefficient for large high-dimensional datasets [45]. For these applications graph-based nearest neighbor search heuristics ∗The full version of the paper can be accessed at https://arxiv.org/abs/2507.13296 †Cornell Tech (ajc473@cornell.edu, btl46@cornell.edu) ‡University of Maryland (laxman@umd.edu, rwen1@umd.edu) §New York University (mlf9579@nyu.edu, cmusco@nyu.edu, ys6378@nyu.edu, torsten.suel@nyu.edu) ¶VMware Research (rob.johnson@broadcom.com) 1Our work only requires that d(pi, pi) = 0 for all i and d(pi, pj) = d(pj , pi) > 0 for i ̸= j (i.e., we assume no repeated data points in the dataset). These conditions are satisfied by any metric, but are much weaker. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited like the Hierarchical Navigable Small World (HNSW) method [44], Vamana/DiskANN [39, 50], Navigating Spreading-out Graphs (NSG) [22], and more [6,21,25,43,45,48,49] have become much more popular. While these graph-based heuristics substantially outperform other (potentially more theoretically sound) techniques in practice, there is surprisingly little underlying theory about their performance. Although the details differ, the core idea behind all of these graph-based search methods is the same, dating back to decades old work on Milgram’s “small world phenomenon” and greedy routing in networks [36,37,46,54]. The basic approach is to preprocess the input dataset by constructing a directed search graph G = (P,E), with a node corresponding to each point pi. To answer a query q, greedy search , or some variant like2, is employed: traverse a path through the graph, starting at an arbitrary node, until a local minimum condition is met. To extend a path at node p, move to p’s neighbor closest to q; the local minimum is achieved (and the path is terminated) when the current p has no neighbors that are closer to q than p is. We then return p. The main computation cost at each step is finding p’s closest neighbor to q, which requires a number of distance computations proportional to p’s degree. A key question in graph-based search is how to choose G, subject to the following competing constraints: Can we construct a graph G whose greedy traversal will yield a nearest neighbor, or good approximate nearest neighbor, of the query point? Can we construct G to be sparse so that memory overhead is minimized and traversing it is fast? We note that these two constraints are indeed in tension: if G were a clique, it would certainly always return the nearest neighbor of a query, but traversing it would take O(n) time for every query. If, on the other hand, G were a constant-degree spanner, greedily traversing G would require just O(1) time per step and the graph would require just O(n) space, but might not return a very good answer to most queries. Our work studies the fundamental question of how to efficiently construct graphs that satisfy the competing constraints. We explain the notions required to make this statement precise in the next section. 1.1 Navigable Search Graphs As discussed above, our goal is to construct a search graph G where greedy search is effective in finding (approximate) nearest neighbors in a point set P . Towards this goal, many different graphs properties have been considered (see [48] for an overview). The most popular such property is navigability. Since convergence to a nearest-neighbor for all possible queries is difficult to guarantee3, navigability demands that, at least if the query q is in P (i.e., it has a nearest neighbor with distance 0), then greedy search on G should return q itself, no matter where the search begins. A standard equivalent definition for navigability is as follows [1]: Definition 1.1 (Navigable Graph). Given point set P and distance function d : P × P → R+, we say that a directed graph G = (P,E) is navigable if, for all pi, pk ∈ P , pi ̸= pk, there exists pj ∈ P such that (pi, pj) ∈ E and d(pj , pk) < d(pi, pk). That is, pi has an out-neighbor, pj, that is closer to pk than pi is. Navigability has been studied in the context of nearest neighbor search for decades [5, 30] and lends its name to modern methods such as HNSW (Hierarchical Navigable Small World) and NSG (Navigating Spreading-out Graphs). Moreover, several other well-studied graph properties are direct generalizations of navigability. For example, the popular DiskANN method [39] aims to construct “α-shortcut reachable graphs” [29], which strengthen Definition 1.1 to require that d(pk, pj) < 1 αd(pi, pj) for some α ≥ 1. Similarly, the τ -monotonicity property of [48] requires d(pk, pj) < d(pi, pj) − τ for some τ ≥ 0. These strengthenings of navigability are interesting because, while they require a denser graph to satisfy, they can lead to strong approximation guarantees. For example, Indyk and Xu recently showed that greedy search in an α-shortcut reachable graph always returns an approximate nearest neighbor p̃ with d(q, p̃) ≤ α+1 α−1 ·minp∈P d(q, p) [29]. Greedy search in τ -monotonic graphs always returns an exact nearest neighbor if minp∈P d(q, p) ≤ τ/2 [48]. Recent work also shows that multiplicative approximation guarantees for general metrics can be obtained when a variant greedy search called Adaptive Beam Search is run on a vanilla navigable graph [1] 2Beam-search, a natural “conservative” version of greedy search is currently the method of choice in practice. 3Despite their practical effectiveness, most graph-based methods do not enjoy worst-case theoretical accuracy guarantees as strong as, for example, methods based on LSH [2]. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Graph navigability is also studied as a natural concept beyond applications to vector search. Milgram’s famous “small-world” experiments from the 1960s and 70s established that social networks are empirically navigable (under an implicit distance function) [46, 51]. This finding prompted follow-up work on understanding the navigability of other real-world networks [8, 20], as well as theoretical work by Kleinberg and others on generative models for graphs and graph evolution that lead to navigability [10,12,23,26,36,37]. 1.2 Efficient Graph Construction Despite the interest in navigability and its practical importance, surprisingly no prior work has addressed one of the most basic algorithmic questions to ask about the property: How efficiently can one compute the sparsest navigable graph for a point set P and distance function d? Note that “sparsest” can be defined in multiple ways: we could ask to minimize maximum degree or average degree. As will become clear in Subsection 2.1, it turns out that a single graph can obtain minimum degree simultaneously for all i, so both these optimization criteria can be achieved simultaneously. Current graph-based nearest neighbor search methods universally employ heuristics that tend to construct “almost” navigable graphs [19]. Some of these heuristics are based on natural relaxations of methods that construct provably navigable graphs, such as the O(n3) time RobustPrune method (with complete candidate set) from DiskANN [39], and related methods proposed in other papers [22, 25, 48]. However, not only are these methods slow (cubic time), but even before relaxation, there is no guarantee that they construct the sparsest navigable graph, or a graph whose sparsity is competitive with the optimum. Indeed, recent work provides examples showing that DiskANN can return a solution Ω̃(n) times worse than optimal [33]. A recent paper by Diwan et al. shows that any set with n unique points admits a navigable graph with Õ(n1.5) edges, and they show how to construct such a graph in Õ(n2) time [19]. However, while it was shown that O(n1.5) is optimal in the worst-case, as observed in follow-up work [1], typical datasets admit far sparser navigable graphs. The algorithm from [19] is not able to take advantage of the fact that a sparser graph might exist for a given P . Similarly, Arya and Mount give an efficient algorithm that for any point set in d-dimensional Euclidean space, constructs a navigable graph with maximum degree 2O(d) [5]. While this is tight in the worst-case, for any particular point set, we could hope to do much better. 1.3 Our Results In this paper, we make progress on the stronger goal stated above of constructing graphs with instance-optimal sparsity. As will be discussed in Section 2, the problem of constructing a minimum sparsity navigable graph can be framed as n different minimum set cover instances, each involving n elements and n sets. Running the standard greedy approximation algorithm for set cover on each instance thus yields an O(logn) approximation in O(n3) time. Our main contribution is to push significantly beyond this bound. In particular, we provide a nearly-quadratic time algorithm: Theorem 1.2. There is a randomized algorithm running in Õ(n2) time4 with high probability that, given any distance function d and point set P in general position5, constructs a navigable graph G in which, with high probability, every vertex has a degree which does not exceed that of the sparsest navigable graph for P by more than a multiplicative c log n factor for a fixed constant c. Theorem 1.2 is proven in Section 4. The result is surprising in that we do not even know how to check if a given graph is navigable in Õ(n2) time. Moreover, under natural complexity-theoretical assumptions, the result is optimal up to logarithmic factors, even when P is in low-dimensional Euclidean space. In particular, any navigable graph G for P must contain a bidirectional edge between the two closest points in P . It has been established that identifying these two points requires Ω(n2−ϵ) time under the Strong Exponential Time Hypothesis (SETH) [32]. Accordingly, we have the following: 4We assume d(·, ·) can be evaluated in O(1) time. Formally, if d(·, ·) takes T time to evaluate, our method runs in Õ(n2) +O(n2 · T ) time. Throughout, we use Õ(m) to denote O(m logc m) for a fixed constant c. 5Formally, we assume d(pi, pj) ̸= d(pi, pk) for any i, j, k. In practice, this can be achieved via an arbitrarily small random perturbation of the points. The assumption is necessary to avoid subtleties that arise in the definition of navigability if there can be exactly tied distances. See Section 3 for details. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Claim 1.3. Assuming the Strong Exponential Time Hypothesis (SETH), any algorithm that, for a set of n points, P , returns a navigable graph with o(n2−ϵ) edges requires Ω(n2−ϵ) time, for any constant ϵ > 0. This claim holds even under the Euclidean distance when P ⊂ RO(logn). Recall that all point sets admit a navigable graph with Õ(n1.5) edges [19]. Claim 1.3 shows that beating quadratic time is unlikely even if we just want to slightly improve on the naive solution of returning a complete graph with O(n2) edges. Obtaining a result with near-minimal sparsity is only harder. Moreover, beating the O(log n) approximation factor in Theorem 1.2 is unlikely. Even when d is a metric, which adds additional structure to the set cover instances underlying the minimum navigable graph problem, we are able to leverage the well-known hardness of approximating set cover [18] to prove in Section 5: Theorem 1.4. For every ε > 0 it is NP-hard to construct a navigable graph G for a given point set P with average out-degree ( 1 3 ln(n)− ε ) ·OPT, where OPT is the average out-degree in the minimum navigable graph for P . This result holds even under Euclidean distance. Beyond our main algorithmic result (Theorem 1.2), we also observe that the related problems of constructing near optimally sparse α-shortcut reachable graphs and τ -monotonic graphs can similarly be framed as n coupled set cover instances. This perspective can be used to obtain faster algorithms for those problems that achieve and O(logn) approximation factor and run in Õ(min(n2.5), n ·OPT), where OPT ≥ n is the sparsity of the optimal solution. Obtaining nearly-quadratic time remains an open question. Concurrent Work. We note that Khanna, Padaki, and Waingarten independently obtain a Õ(n · OPT) time algorithm for constructing a near optimally sparse α-shortcut reachable graphs [33]. Like our work, their approach is based on connecting the problem to set cover. They also define and describe efficient algorithms for an interesting bi-criteria variant of the problem. 2 Technical Overview 2.1 Sparsest Navigable Graph as Minimum Set Cover As a starting point towards understand-ing better algorithms, we first observe that constructing a navigable graph amounts to solving n separate minimum set cover instances, one for each node. For the instance corresponding to node pi, the elements that need to be covered are all other points pk, k ̸= i. We also have one set, Si→j , for each pj , j ̸= i, which corresponds to adding an edge from i to j in G. Si→j contains any point pk such that d(pj , pk) < d(pi, pk). I.e., to cover an element pk, we need to have an edge from pi either directly to pk, or to some node that gets us closer to pk. Choosing the smallest number of sets that covers all elements amounts to choosing the smallest number of out-edges for pi that satisfy the navigability requirements of Definition 1.1. See Figure 2.1 for an illustration of the set cover instances. The set cover formulation reinforces that, while navigability is usually thought of as a “global property” about routing in G, it can be viewed as a collection of purely “local” conditions: the out-edges of node pi can be chosen optimally without considering out-edges for any other nodes in the graph. Moreover, while we are not aware of prior work that leverages the connection to set cover (beyond the concurrent work of [33]) it immediately leads to an approach for constructing a navigable graph with near minimal sparsity: run any black-box approximation algorithm for each of the set cover instances. The best known approximation ratio of O(log n) can be achieved by the standard greedy algorithm in time linear in the total size of the sets [13]. Implementing this idea naively amounts to O(n2) time per instance in our setting, for a total complexity of O(n3) time to construct a navigable graph where the degree of every node is at most O(log n) higher than optimal. By simultaneously obtaining near-minimum degree for every node, it follows that the greedy algorithm produces a graph G whose maximum and average degree are within O(log n) of optimal. 2.2 Algorithmic Techniques Our algorithm that establishes Theorem 1.2 relies on three key ingredients to push beyond the O(n3) baseline obtained via greedy set cover. These ingredients are summarized below. Efficient Query Access. We first observe that, instead of treating navigable graph construction as n disjoint set cover instances, we can obtain a faster runtime by taking advantage of the fact that these instances are highly correlated. Concretely, we can jointly preprocess the instances in O(n2 log n) time to Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited p1 p5 p2 p3 p4 p6 S1→3 p1 p5 p2 p3 p4 p6 S1→5 p1 p5 p2 p3 p4 p6 S1→6 Figure 2.1: Illustration of the navigability set cover problem corresponding to node p1. Each image shows the set, S1→j , for a different choice of out-neighbor j. S1→j contains all pk for which d(pj , pk) < d(pi, pk). Constructing a navigable graph which has the fewest out-edges from node 1 is equivalent to solving the minimum set cover problem for this instance. Constructing a navigable graph with the fewest total number of edges is equivalent to solving the minimum set cover problem for all n different problem instances. support natural “query access” to the set cover problem, which obviates the need to explicitly construct each set cover instance (write down each set and its elements), as doing so would already require Ω(n3) time. Specifically, for each point, we construct a list of all other points, sorted in increasing order of distance from that point. Doing so takes O(n log n) time per data point, so O(n2 log n) time total. From these lists, we can immediately answer what is called a “SetOf” query in the literature on sublinear-time set cover algorithms [27,47,56]: concretely, for any element pk, we can return in O(1) time the number of sets, q, that cover the element, and the identity of the ℓth set covering pk for any ℓ ≤ q. To do so, we simply look at the sorted list corresponding to the point pk. For the set cover instance corresponding to node i, the number of points before pi in pk’s list is q, as this is the number of points j for which d(pj , pk) < d(pi, pk). Moreover, the ℓth set that covers pk is simply the set corresponding to the ℓth element of the list. In addition to SetOf queries, presorting our data also allows us to easily answer “Membership” queries in O(1) time: to check if pk ∈ Si→j , simply check if pj precedes pi in the sorted list corresponding to pk. With the ability to answer SetOf and Membership queries in O(1) time, we can immediately obtain faster algorithms by turning to work on sublinear-time set cover algorithms, which assumes similar query access [24,27,38]. Specifically, a modification of an algorithm from [38] can be shown to provide an O(logn) approximation to an unweighted set cover problem with n elements, m sets, and optimal solution OPT in time Õ(m+n ·OPT) [52]. Applying this result separately to each node, we can obtain an O(log n) approximation to the sparsest navigable graph in time Õ (n · OPT), which is always bounded by Õ(n2.5) since every point set has a navigable graph with OPT = O(n1.5) (see [19] or Section 3). Recall that our final goal (Theorem 1.2) is a Õ(n2) time algorithm, but Õ(n2.5) is already a significant improvement on the naive O(n3). Similar bounds can be obtained for constructing sparse α-shortcut reachable and τ -monotonic graphs, which as discussed, are also of interest for graph-based search [29,48,50]. These problems can also be framed as collections of set cover problems, and SetOf/Membership queries can likewise be implemented in O(1) time with slightly different preprocessing. Overall, we obtain the following result, formally proven in Section 6: Corollary 2.1. For any α ≥ 1, let OPTα be the minimum number of edges in an α-shortcut reachable graph for a point set P and distance function d. For a fixed constant c, there is an algorithm that constructs an α-shortcut reachable graph with ≤ c logn · OPTα edges in Õ ( min(n2.5, n · OPTα)) ) time. Similarly, for any τ ≥ 0, let OPTτ be the minimum number of edges in a τ -monotonic graph for P . There is an algorithm that constructs a τ -monotonic graph with ≤ c log n · OPTτ edges in Õ ( min(n2.5, n · OPTτ )) ) time. Greedy Simulation. More work is required to obtain the Õ(n2) time result of Theorem 1.2. In particular, this result takes advantage of additional structure in the set cover instances that arise from the minimum navigable graph problem. To do so, we introduce a new, purely combinatorial algorithm for set cover with SetOf/Membership queries, and then show how to accelerate our new method with careful edge preprocessing. Concretely, our approach is based on directly simulating the natural greedy algorithm for set cover, which repeatedly selects the set that covers the largest number of uncovered elements. This differs from the Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited sublinear time method from [38], which is based on solving the fractional set cover problem and rounding the solution. To simulate the greedy method, we uniformly sample uncovered points and run SetOf queries to record what sets those points are contained in. Doing so allows us to estimate the number of uncovered elements in any set. These estimates can then be used to choose a near-optimal set at each iteration, yielding the same O(log n) approximation that is obtained when the truly optimal greedy set is chosen. This greedy simulation is reminiscent of an approach from Nguyen and Onak [47]. It also bears high-level similarity to an algorithm of Indyk et al. [27], which samples random elements and selects multiple sets at once by finding a near-optimal set cover for those elements. Edge Preprocessing. A first optimization to our greedy simulation method comes from sampling roughly OPT sets uniformly at random to add to the set cover for each node, where OPT is the size of the minimum set cover for the node.6 Doing so does not significantly impact our final approximation factor, and ensures that all elements contained in ≳ m/OPT sets are covered after this first sampling step (here m is the total number of sets, which is n− 1 for the navigability problem). As a consequence, the cost of recording what sets a randomly sampled element is contained in can be bounded by ≲ m/OPT instead of the naive m. We note that the same preprocessing technique is used in the sublinear time set cover algorithm of [27]. Already, this simple optimization allows our greedy simulation to match the efficiency of current state-of-the-art sublinear time algorithms for set cover – see Section A for more details. Moreover, adding random edges can lead to an even faster algorithm in the minimum navigable graph setting. Indeed, if the optimum degree of every node is roughly the same – i.e., O(OPT∗/n), where OPT∗ is the number of total edges in the sparsest navigable graph – one can prove that the random edges cover all but O(n3/OPT∗) elements across all n set cover instances. The number of uncovered elements plays a direct role in the running time of our algorithm, and this bound suffices to show that the total running time for all n instances is at most Õ(n2). However, more thought is required when the optimal degrees vary (they could by a lot). In this case, we are unable to make strong claims about the absolute number of elements covered by random edges. We deal with this challenge by introducing an additional preprocessing step: essentially, we group nodes with similar optimum degree and connect them by a clique. The effect of doing so is that any pj not in the clique is covered in all but one of the set cover instances corresponding to nodes in the clique. In particular, pj is covered in all instances besides the one corresponding to its nearest neighbor in the clique. We defer details to Section 4, but roughly speaking, this clique forming technique allows for fine-grained control over the number of uncovered elements in each instance after preprocessing, yielding our final Õ(n2) time result. A side consequence of this final technique (discussed further in Section 3) is that it actually allows us to prove a slightly tighter existence result for navigable graphs: we show that any point set in general position admits a navigable graph with O(n3/2) edges. Prior work achieved a bound of O(n3/2 √ log n) [19]. 3 Preliminaries Set Cover Notation. Central to our approach is the set cover formulation of the sparsest navigable graph problem. We begin by reiterating notation introduced in the introduction. Formally, for each point pi ∈ P = {p1, . . . , pn}, we have a set cover instance Ii = (P \ {pi},Fi) with elements P \ {pi} and sets Fi = {Si→j : j ̸= i}, where the set Si→j contains all pk ∈ P \ {pi} such that d(pj , pk) < d(pi, pk). Our solution to the set cover instances will be a set of out-neighborhoods N1, . . . , Nn, one for each point. If pj ∈ Ni this indicates that set Si→j was selected in our solution to Ii. These neighborhoods comprise our final navigable graph, G. We let OPTi denote the minimum size solution to set cover instance Ii and OPT∗ = ∑n i=1 OPTi denote the minimum number of edges in a navigable graph for point set P . Distance Functions. As discussed, our algorithms work with any distance function d, where we define: Definition 3.1 (Distance Function). A distance function d : P × P → R≥0 for a point set P = {p1, . . . , pn} is any function such that d(pi, pi) = 0 for all i and d(pj , pi) = d(pi, pj) > 0 for all j ̸= i. Importantly, we do not require triangle inequality. Throughout, we make the mild assumption that the point set P is in “general position” meaning that 1) it contains no repeated data points and 2) for all i, j, k, 6While we do not know OPT in advance, it can be estimated via an exponential grid search. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited d(pi, pj) ̸= d(pi, pk). These conditions are essentially always true in practice, or can be ensured by adding arbitrarily small random perturbations to the data. While assuming general position is not strictly necessary, as in prior work [1], doing so greatly simplifies the definition of navigability. In particular, Definition 1.1 only captures the usual definition of navigability (that greedy search always converges for a query q ∈ P ) if there is a strict ordering on the distances d(pi, p1), . . . , d(pi, pn). Otherwise “greedy search” is not well-defined unless we introduce a tie-breaking rule that determines where to move if two neighbors are equally close to a query. Avoiding the notation and corner-casing required to handle tie-breaking simplifies our results. Other Notation. Throughout, we use “with high probability” to mean with probability 1 − 1/nc for an arbitrarily large constant c (the specific choice of which will impact other constant terms in the statement). 3.1 The Distance-Based Permutation Matrix and Fast Query Access As in work on existential results for navigability [19], it will be helpful to think about our navigability problem as represented in a “distance-based permutation matrix”, Π, with n rows and n columns. The ith row contains the points in P sorted in increasing order of their distance from pi. See Figure 3.1 for an example. The value of the distance-based permutation matrix is that it only takes O(n2 log n) time to construct, yet allows us to efficiently query all n set cover instances I1, . . . , In. In particular, we have the following: Claim 3.2. Given a set of n points, P , and a distance function d that can be evaluated in T time for any pi, pj ∈ P , we can construct in O(n2T + n2 log n) time a data structure that answers the following queries in constant time for any pi, pj , pk ∈ {1, . . . , n}
MemberOf (pi, pj , pk): Returns Yes if pk ∈ Si→j, No otherwise.
FreqOf(pi, pk): Returns the number of sets in Ii that pk is a member of, i.e., |{j : pk ∈ Si→j}|
SetOf(pi, pk, ℓ): Returns the ℓth set (according to some fixed order) that pk is a member of in Ii, or Null if ℓ > FreqOf(pi, pk). In other words, return the ℓth element of {j : pk ∈ Si→j}. Our algorithms will only ever use SetOf queries to return all values of j such that pk ∈ Si→j . This can be done in linear time by repeatedly issuing SetOf queries for ℓ = 1, 2, . . . until a Null value is returned. Proof. Data Structure. We first compute and store all pairwise distances in O(n2T ) time. We then sort n lists to build the distance-based permutation matrix, Π in O(n2 log n) time. We then precompute and store the rank of each pj in the ith row of Π, for all i. We denote this rank by π−1 i (j). This takes O(n2) time. Answering Queries. To answer a MemberOf query, we simply return Yes if π−1 k (j) < π−1 k (i) – i.e., pj precedes pi in the kth row of Π. We return No otherwise. To answer a FreqOf query, we return the number of items that precede pi in row k, i.e. π−1 k (i)− 1. To answer a SetOf query, we return Null if ℓ ≥ π−1 k (i). Otherwise, we return Πk,ℓ, the ℓth entry in the kth row of Π. All operations take O(1) time. 3.2 Pre-processing via Random Edges and Arbitrary Cliques As discussed in Subsection 2.2, a key component of our algorithm is to reduce the cost of running a greedy set cover method by “pre-processing” the navigability problem by adding a small number of less carefully chosen edges that nevertheless aid in covering many elements. We do so in two ways: by adding random edges and adding random cliques. We discuss basic properties of these strategies below. 3.2.1 The Power of Random Edges A first observation, which is also used in prior work on sublinear time set cover [27], is that adding random sets to instances I1, . . . , In will cover all elements contained in many sets, with high probability. In our setting, this corresponds to adding random out-neighbors to each node. We have the following basic claim: Claim 3.3. Suppose we sample β indices j1, . . . , jβ uniformly at random from {1, . . . , n} \ i and add Si→j1 , . . . , Si→jβ to our solution, Ni, for set cover instance Ii. Then for any pk contained in at least c ln(n) · nβ sets Si→j, we have that pk ∈ Si→j1 ∪ · · · ∪ Si→jβ with probability > 1− 1/nc. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Figure 3.1: An example distance-based permutation matrix, Π, for a data set in two-dimensional Euclidean space. The ith row contains all points in P sorted in increasing order of their distance from pi. Π’s first row is [p1, p5, p4, p6, p2p3] since d(p1, p1) < d(p1, p5) < . . . < d(p1, p3). Π can be used to quickly identify what sets a node pk is contained in for a particular set cover instance Ii: if pk ∈ Si→j , then pj will lie to the left of pi in the kth row of Π. This property is illustrated for a particular set S1→3 = {p2, p3, p4}, highlighted in red. As we can see, p3 lies to the left of p1 in rows 2, 3, 4 in Π, and to the right in all other rows. Proof. We can assume Si→j1 , . . . , Si→jβ are drawn with replacement, as the probability that pk is covered is only higher if they are drawn without replacement. Since pk is contained in z ≥ cn ln(n)/β sets, for any randomly-chosen ℓ, Pr[pk ∈ Si→ℓ] = z n−1 . Accordingly, we can bound: Pr[pk /∈ Si→j1 ∪ . . . , Si→jβ ] ≤ ( 1− z n− 1 )β < ( 1− c lnn β )β ≤ ( 1 e )c lnn = 1 nc . An implication of Claim 3.3 is that, if we add β random edges to every node in our graph, then with high probability, for all instances Ii, the only elements remaining to be covered are contained in ≤ n/β sets. This property will be critical in our efficient simulation of the greedy set cover algorithm, which requires sampling a random uncovered element and recording all sets it is contained in. Due to the additional structure of the navigable graph problem, adding random sets actually guarantees another property that does not hold for arbitrary collections of set cover instances: we can ensure that the total number of uncovered elements across all set cover instances is significantly reduced. Corollary 3.4. Suppose that for each set cover instance Ii, as in Claim 3.3, we add β sets uniformly at random to our solution, Ni. For each i ∈ [n], let Ui denote the set of all uncovered points pk that are not contained in any of the random sets chosen for instance Ii. Then with probability > 1− 1/nc−2, n∑ i=1 |Ui| < c log n · n 2 β . Proof. By Claim 3.3 and a union bound, we have that, simultaneously for all i, all pk contained in ≥ c log(n) · nβ sets in Ii are in at least one of the randomly-chosen sets for instance i. So Ui only contains elements in < c log(n) · nβ sets. Referring to the distance-based permutation matrix, we notice that there are n · (c log(n) · n β − 2) such elements across all instances: these elements are exactly those contained in columns proceeding column c log(n) · n β in the matrix (excluding the first column). It follows that∑n i=1 |Ui| < c log n · n 2 β . 3.2.2 The Power of Cliques Interestingly, a result similar to Corollary 3.4 can be obtained without randomness, but rather by adding arbitrary cliques to our graph. In particular, we can argue that, if we add a clique over a subset of nodes, K, then the total number of uncovered elements across all Ii for i ∈ K is bounded. Formally, we have: Claim 3.5. Suppose we select points K ⊂ P and add a clique over those points to G. I.e., for each pi ∈ K, we add K \ {pi} to pi’s out-neighborhood, Ni. Then for any pk /∈ K, for all but possibly a single Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Figure 3.2: A partial illustration of the graph construction used to prove Theorem 3.6. We begin by partitioning our points into O( √ n) arbitrary groups of size O( √ n). An example group, K, is illustrated in green. We connect all points in K via a clique and for every point pk /∈ K, we draw an edge from pk’s nearest neighbor in K to pk. By Claim 3.5, this yields a navigable graph. The total number of edges is O(n3/2). pi ∈ K, pk ∈ Si→j for some pj ∈ Ni. That is, pk is covered in all set cover instances corresponding to nodes in the clique, except at most one. Let Ui contain all pk not covered in instance Ii by edges in G. It follows that: ∑ i∈K |Ui| ≤ n− |K|. Proof. Let pℓ = argminpi∈K d(pi, pk). For all pi ∈ K, i ̸= ℓ we have that pk is contained in Si→ℓ, which we added when forming the clique. So pk can only be uncovered in instance Iℓ. There are n − |K| points not in the clique, hence at most n− |K| uncovered elements across all instances. An immediate consequence of Claim 3.5 is that, if we partition our points into n/β sets of size β, and add a clique for each of those sets, then we only add O(nβ) edges to the graph, but cover all but (n−β)·n/β < n2/β elements across all n set cover instances. This matches what was obtained above for random edge deletions. We will use a slightly finer-grained version of the statement in our proof of Theorem 1.2 in Section 4. While a fairly simple observation, the argument above can actually be used to tighten the previous best upper bound of 2n3/2 √ lnn on the number of edges in the minimum navigable graph for an arbitrary distance function and point set (in general position) [19]. In particular, simply partition the points into ⌈ √ n⌉ arbitrary groups of size ≤ √ n. For each group K, add a clique between the nodes in that group. For any pk /∈ K, add an edge from pℓ = argminpi∈K d(pi, pk). This yields a navigable graph, and we add at most |K|(|K| − 1) + n− |K| = |K|2 + n− 2|K| edges per group K, for a total of (2n− 2 √ n) · √ n edges when n is a perfect square. It can be verified that essentially the same bound holds for non-square n, yielding: Theorem 3.6. There is a deterministic algorithm that, given any point set P of size n and distance function d, constructs a navigable graph on P with at most 2n3/2 − n edges in O(n2) time. 4 A Nearly Quadratic Time Algorithm In this section, we prove Theorem 1.2 by presenting an algorithm that returns an O(log n)-approximation to the minimum navigable graph for any set of n points and any distance function in Õ(n2) time. 4.1 Vote Cover Subroutine Our algorithm is based on efficiently simulating the standard greedy algorithm for each of the n set cover instances that corresponds to the navigable graph problem (see Section 3). Our general simulation routine, which we call VoteCover, is detailed in Algorithm 4.1. VoteCover takes as input a source point pi ∈ P and a set of points U ⊆ P \ {pi} to be covered. It returns a set of neighbors for pi that cover U . The reason we specify a set of points U instead of asking to cover all of P \ {pi} is because VoteCover will ultimately be combined with “edge preprocessing” techniques (discussed in Section 2) that cover some points in advance to reduce runtime. The algorithm also takes in a positive parameter γ that controls its success probability, as well as an optional size limit ℓ, which specifies an early-termination condition; that is, we allow VoteCover to return Fail in lieu of a cover Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited of size > 2ℓ. Early termination will also be helpful when combining VoteCover with the preprocessing techniques. Our main result on the algorithm is as follows: Theorem 4.1. Given a point set P preprocessed as in Claim 3.2, a source point pi ∈ P , a subset of points U ⊆ P \ {pi} to cover, a size limit ℓ, and parameter γ, Algorithm 4.1 returns either Fail or a set Q ⊆ P with |Q| ≤ ℓ such that, for any pk ∈ U , there is some pj ∈ Q satisfying pk ∈ Si→j. The algorithm runs in Õ((f + |U |) · min(ℓ, γOPTi)), where f is the maximum number of sets Si→j that any pk ∈ U is contained in and OPTi is the optimal cover size for set cover instance Ii, as defined in Section 3. Moreover, if ℓ ≥ min(n, (2γ+5)OPTi lnn), then Algorithm 4.1 successfully returns a set with probability ≥ 1−1/nγ/4−2. Algorithm 4.1 VoteCover(P, pi, U ⊆ P, ℓ) Input: A dataset P pre-processed as in Claim 3.2, a source point pi, a set U ⊆ P of uncovered elements in set cover instance Ii, a size limit ℓ, and a parameter γ > 0 that controls success probability. Output: A set of at most ℓ neighbors for pi that cover U , or Fail. 1: Initialize the cover C ← ∅ and the voter pool V ← ∅. 2: Initialize votes← array of n empty sets. 3: while |U | > 0 do 4: Select uniformly random pk from U . Set U ← U \ {pk}, V ← V ∪ {pk} ▷ Promote a random uncovered point pk to the voter pool. 5: if |V |+ |C| > ℓ then return Fail 6: for each Si→j containing pk do 7: votes[j]← votes[j] ∪ {pk} ▷ pk votes for all Si→j containing it. 8: if |votes[j]| ≥ γ lnn then 9: C ← C ∪ {pj} ▷ Add Si→j to the cover if it receives enough votes. 10: if |V |+ |C| > ℓ then return Fail 11: for each pv ∈ votes[j] do ▷ Remove votes of all voters for Si→j , which are now covered. 12: for each Si→u containing pv do 13: votes[u]← votes[u] \ {pv} 14: V ← V \ {pu} 15: for each pv ∈ U do ▷ Remove all points covered by Si→j from U . 16: if pv ∈ Si→j then 17: U ← U \ {pv} 18: break 19: return C ∪ V The standard greedy set cover algorithm initializes an empty cover and sequentially adds the set that covers the most uncovered points. Since we do not have explicit access to sets in our setting and cannot efficiently compute how many elements they cover, VoteCover (Algorithm 4.1) approximates this process by randomly sampling “voters” from all uncovered points, which “vote” for all sets Si→j that cover them. In the pseudocode, we use C to represent the cover, which formally contains out-neighbors, pj , to add to pi. If pj ∈ C, then we have selected set Si→j for set cover instance Ii. We use V to denote the set of uncovered points that have been chosen as voters. To select a high-coverage set, we sample an uncovered point from the set U , move it to V , and have it vote once for every set which contains it. These sets can be efficiently obtained via SetOf queries. We record the individual votes in an array called votes, where votes[j] is a set holding the ids of all voters currently voting for Si→j , i.e., votes[j] = V ∩ Si→j . When repeating this process many times, we expect the sets containing the most uncovered points to receive the most votes. The number of voters we need to sample in order for this to be a likely event depends on the amount of coverage these sets offer, so rather than choose a fixed number of samples, we instead sample points until some set reaches a threshold of γ lnn votes. At this point, we are confident that it has near maximal coverage among all remaining sets, so we add the set to C. γ is a constant whose effect will Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited become apparent in our analysis, but in brief, a larger choice of γ loosens our upper bound on the size of the returned set in exchange for increasing the probability that the set satisfies that bound. Rather than restart the voting from scratch each time we choose a set, which would be costly, we continue from the current state, keeping all votes except those cast by voters that are now covered. Since all newly covered voters are contained in votes[j], they are easy to identify and remove. We also update our set of uncovered points, U , using MemberOf queries to identify and remove all points covered by the chosen set. We then continue to add highly-voted sets to C until U is empty. At this point, there may still be active voters in V whose sets never received enough votes to be chosen. We finish by explicitly covering each of these voters with the set corresponding to itself. We will prove that the number of such sets we need is small. 4.1.1 Correctness and Approximation Factor We begin by analyzing the accuracy of Algo-rithm 4.1. Specifically, we show that if ℓ is chosen sufficiently large, then the algorithm returns a cover of size O(OPTi log n) with high probability. Lemma 4.2. VoteCover(P, pi, U, ℓ, γ) either returns Fail or a cover of U with size ≤ ℓ. Furthermore, if ℓ ≥ min(n, (2γ + 5)OPTi lnn), then it returns a cover with probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2 Proof outline. We first show that, if the failure conditions on Lines 5 and 10 are never reached, the algorithm returns a cover of U with size O(OPTi log n), with high probability. To do so, we prove that V always contains a uniform random sample of the remaining uncovered points. As discussed above, this allows us to argue that any pj satisfying the condition on Line 9 covers nearly as many uncovered points as the best choice of set. Adapting the standard analysis of greedy set cover, we conclude that the algorithm terminates after adding at most O(OPTi log n) sets to C. In addition, we show that the size of V never exceeds O(OPTi log n) with high probability, so C ∪ V must have size at most O(OPTi log n), proving the first claim of the theorem. For the formal proof, we begin with some basic definitions and facts. Definition 4.3. Given an initial set U ⊆ P {pi} of points to cover and a set C ⊆ P {pi} corresponding to a partial cover, let UC denote U \ ⋃ pc∈C Si→c. I.e., UC contains all points not covered by C. At the start of the algorithm, when C = ∅, UC = U . Each additional neighbor added to C shrinks the size of UC . The task of VoteCover is to find a small C such that UC = ∅. Claim 4.4. Algorithm 4.1 maintains the invariant that U and V are disjoint sets such that U ∪V = UC . Proof. This is clearly true at the start of the algorithm when V = ∅, C = ∅, and thereby UC = U . As the algorithm executes, elements are never added to U or V other than during a movement from U to V on Line 4, which has no effect U ∪ V . Finally, points are only every removed from U or V when the set corresponding to some out-neighbor, pj , is added to C. In this case, we can see that exactly those points covered by pj are removed from U (Lines 15-17) and V (Lines 11-14). Thus the invariant is maintained. Lemma 4.5. If Algorithm 4.1 does not return Fail, then it returns a cover of U . Proof. If Algorithm 4.1 is never interrupted by either failure condition on Lines 5 or 10, it can only return on Line 19. This line is reached under the condition that |U | = 0. Since each iteration of the main loop removes at least one element from U , and elements are never added to U , we will eventually terminate. Morever, it is clear that C∪V is a valid cover of U when the algorithm terminates. In particular, by Claim 4.4, if |U | = 0, then UC = V . I.e., the only points not covered by C are those in V . Since d(pk, pk) = 0 < d(pi, pk) for any pk ∈ V , adding these points to the cover ensures they are also covered. Claim 4.6. Algorithm 4.1 maintains the invariant that V is a uniform sample (without replacement) of UC . Proof. This is trivially true at the start of the algorithm when V = ∅. During the algorithm, the only additions to V are randomly chosen points from U , which by Claim 4.4, equals UC \V . Thus, these random additions preserve V being a random sample of UC . On the other hand, points are only removed from V Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited when some pj is added to C. The points removed are exactly those in Si→j . If V is uniform sample of UC , then V \ Si→j is a uniform random sample of UC \ Si→j = U C∪{pj}, so the invariant continues to hold. Claim 4.7. For any C ⊆ P , there is always some Si→j that contains at least a 1 OPTi fraction of UC . Proof. Let COPT be an optimal cover for P \ {pi}. We have that UC ⊆ ⋃ pj∈COPT (Sj→c ∩ UC), which implies that ∑ pj∈COPT |Si→j∩UC | ≥ |UC |. Some pj ∈ COPT must therefore satisfy |Sj→c∩UC | ≥ 1 OPTi |UC |. Lemma 4.8. With probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−1, |V | never exceeds 2γOPTi lnn. Proof. By Claim 4.7, there is always some set S∗ = Si→j∗ containing at least a 1 OPTi fraction of UC , for any C ⊆ P . Thus, any randomly-selected point from UC has at least a 1 OPTi probability of being contained in S∗. If our random sample V ⊆ UC contains 2γOPTi lnn points, then by a standard Chernoff bound (e.g., [53]), S∗ will contain at least γ lnn (the vote threshold from Algorithm 4.1) points from V with probability at least: Pr[|S∗ ∩ V | ≥ γ lnn] = 1− Pr[|S∗ ∩ V | < (1/2)(2γ lnn)] ≥ 1− e−(1/2)2γ lnn ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4 That is, with high probability, S∗ reaches the vote threshold by the time there are 2γOPTi lnn active voters. Subsequently, its voters are removed from V . Taking a union bound over at most n states of V , we see that with probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−1, V contains at most 2γOPTi lnn points at any one time. Claim 4.9. With probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2, every set added to C contains at least a 1 5OPTi fraction of UC . Proof. When the |V | ≤ 2γOPTi lnn, the probability that a set Ssmall = Si→jsmall containing less than a 1 5OPTi fraction of UC receives more than γ lnn votes can be bounded, again by a Chernoff bound, by Pr[|Ssmall ∩ V | ≥ γ lnn] ≤ e−(3/2)2(4/35)γ lnn < 1/nγ/4. Taking a union bound over at most n−1 sets and across at most n states of V yields that all sets containing less than a 1 5OPTi fraction of UC simultaneously fall short of the required γ lnn votes with probability at least 1 − (n − 1)/nγ/4−1 = 1 − 1/nγ/4−2 + 1/nγ/4−1. Further union bounding with the ≥ 1 − 1/nγ/4−1 probability that the number of active voters never exceeds 2γOPTi lnn, as given by Lemma 4.8, we have that with probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2, all sets added to C contain at least a 1 5OPTi fraction of UC . Lemma 4.10. With probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2, |C| never exceeds 5OPTi lnn. Proof. Let C0 = ∅ and let Ci denote the state of C after adding the ith set. By Claim 4.9, with probability ≥ 1−1/nγ/4−2, |UCk+1 | ≤ (1− 1 5OPTi )|UCk | for all k. Under this condition, if we let t = 5OPTi lnn, then |UCt | ≤ |UC0 | ( 1− 1 5OPTi )t ≤ (n− 1) ( 1− 1 5OPTi )5OPTi lnn ≤ (n− 1)e− lnn < 1. So, the algorithm terminates having added at most 5OPTi lnn sets to C. We are now ready to prove our main result on approximation: Proof of Lemma 4.2. By Lemma 4.5, VoteCover (Algorithm 4.1) always returns either a cover of U or Fail. By Lemmas 4.8 and 4.10, |V | remains ≤ 2γOPTi lnn and |C| remains ≤ 5OPTi lnn with probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2. Therefore, if ℓ ≥ min(n, (2γ+5)OPTi lnn), with probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2, neither failure condition on Lines 5 and 10 is ever satisfied. Thus, VoteCover returns a cover of U , and the returned cover, C ∪ V , has size ≤ min(n, |C|+ |V |) ≤ min(n, (2γ + 5)OPTi lnn) ≤ ℓ. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited 4.1.2 Running Time With Lemma 4.2 in place, we next analyze the running time of Algorithm 4.1. Lemma 4.11. VoteCover(P, pi, U, ℓ, γ) runs in Õ((f + |U |)min(ℓ, γOPTi)) time with probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2, where f is the maximum number of sets Si→j that any pk ∈ U is contained in. Proof. Any work outside of the main while-loop trivially takes O(n) time. Lines 4-5 also take O(n) time total, because each iteration removes one element from U , which is initialized with size at most |U | ≤ n elements. So, we focus on analyzing Lines 6-18. We divide the cost into four parts. Cost of casting votes, specifically by voters that are removed from V (Line 7). By Line 8, the number of voters removed from V for a given pj is exactly γ lnn. By Lemma 4.10, the number of sets added to C is at most 5OPTi lnn with probability ≥ 1− 1/nγ/4−2 if ℓ is sufficiently large, or ℓ otherwise. Thus, the total number of voters removed from V is at most O(min(ℓ, γOPTi log n) log n). Each such voter casts at most f votes, which requires f SetOf queries, each running in O(1) time. So the total cost of recording votes by voters that are at some point removed from V is O(f ·min(ℓ, γOPTi log n) log n). Cost of casting votes by voters that remain in V (Line 7). By Lemma 4.8, the number of voters in V at any time is at most O(γOPTi logn) if ℓ is sufficiently large, or ℓ otherwise. Each such voter cast at most f votes, so the total number of votes cast by voters that are never removed from V is O(f ·min(ℓ, γOPTi log n)). Cost of undoing votes (Lines 9-14). When a voter is removed from V , the cost of removing its votes is iden-tical to the initial cost of adding them in the first place, which is bounded by O(f ·min(ℓ, γOPTi log n) log n). Cost of removing covered points from U (Lines 15-17). Let m be the initial size of the input U . Each time some pj is added to C, we do a full scan over U and use membership queries to remove any points covered by Si→j . If the algorithm hits the limit ℓ of sets added, it is easy to see that this takes at most O(mℓ) time. If not, recall from Claim 4.9 that each set added to C covers at least a 1 5OPTi fraction of UC = U ∪ V with high probability. It follows that after adding the tth set, the cost of the corresponding scan over U is O(m · (1− 1 5OPTi )t). Thus, the cost of removing covered points from U is bounded by O ( 5OPTi lnn∑ t=0 m ( 1− 1 5OPTi )t ) = O ( ∞∑ t=0 m ( 1− 1 5OPTi )t ) = O (mOPTi) . So, the total cost of removing covered points is at most O(m ·min(ℓ,OPTi)). Combining all costs, we conclude that Algorithm 4.1 runs in time Õ((f + |U |)min(ℓ, γOPTi)). Theorem 4.1 follows as an immediate consequence of Lemma 4.11 and Lemma 4.2. 4.1.3 Graph Construction in Õ(n2.5) On its own, VoteCover already implies a simple Õ(n2.5) time algorithm for constructing a near optimally sparse navigable graph. This initial result improves on a naive O(n3) time implementation of greedy set cover and motivates our stronger Õ(n2) time result from Theorem 1.2. Theorem 4.12. There is an algorithm that, given a set of points P and a distance function d, runs in Õ(n ·OPT∗) ≤ Õ(n2.5) time and returns a navigable graph G on P with probability ≥ 1− 1 nc for any constant c ≥ 1, in which each node pi ∈ P has O(OPTi logn) out-neighbors. Recall that OPT∗ = ∑n i=1 OPTi and we always have OPT∗ ≤ 2n1.5 by Theorem 3.6. So, while we obtain a worst case bound of Õ(n2.5), the result is output sensitive, improving if G has a sparser navigable graph. Theorem 4.12 is obtained by simply running VoteCover independently for each pi, as show in Algorithm 4.2. Proof of Theorem 4.12. By Lemma 4.2, we can invoke VoteCover on each pi to successfully cover P \ {pi} with at most O(OPTi · logn) neighbors simply by setting ℓ to n. All calls to VoteCover Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Algorithm 4.2 SimpleSparsestNavigableGraph(P, d) Input: A point set P of size n and a distance function d. A constant c ≥ 1 that controls failure probability. Output: A navigable graph on P . 1: G← (P,∅) 2: Preprocess P as in Claim 3.2 3: for each pi ∈ P do 4: Ni ← VoteCover (P, pi, P \ {pi}, n, 4c+ 12) 5: return G simultaneously succeed with probability at least 1− 1/nγ/4−3 where γ = 4c+ 12, which is at least 1− 1/nc for n ≥ 2. Because there are n sets Si→j for each i, the frequency of any point in any set cover instance is trivially at most n. Moreover, the size of |U | = P \ {pi} is n − 1 for any i. Thus, by Lemma 4.11, the call to VoteCover on point pi runs in Õ((n+n)min(ℓ, cOPTi)) = Õ(nOPTi) time. Therefore, Algorithm 4.2 runs in Õ( ∑ pi∈P nOPTi) = Õ(nOPT∗) time. As explained in Section 6, a nice property of Algorithm 4.2 is that it readily applies to related graph construction problems, such as constructing near optimally sparse α-shortcut reachability and τ -monotonic graphs. We only require minor modifications to how we preprocess P to support set access queries. 4.2 Sparse Navigable Graph Construction in Õ(n2) To obtain the improved Õ(n2) running time of our main result, Theorem 1.2, we make two major additions to the algorithm discussed above. These additions are summarized in Subsection 3.2. For each set cover instance Ii, we choose a value βi and prepend the call of VoteCover on Ii with the following two procedures: We add βi random edges from pi to other points in P . This covers any points in instance Ii that are contained in many sets, which would otherwise cast many votes in VoteCover. We assign pi to an arbitrary group K ⊆ P with size βi and add a bi-directional clique on K. Doing so bounds the number of uncovered points across all set cover instances corresponding to nodes in K. After these preprocessing steps, we remove all points covered by the edges added, and then run VoteCover on the remaining points. We discuss how preprocessing impacts the running time of VoteCover in Section 4.2.1. Then, in Section 4.2.2, we explain how to select βi for each Ii in order to obtain an O(logn)-approximate minimum navigable graph in Õ(n2) time. 4.2.1 Random Edges and Cliques In Claim 3.3 we showed that, if we add β random out-neighbors to pi, then any point contained in ≥ cn ln(n)/β sets in set cover instances Ii is covered with probability > 1− 1/nc. We leverage this fact to allow VoteCover to run faster. Claim 4.13. If R ⊆ P is a uniform random sample of size β, then running VoteCover on point pi with size limit ℓ, sufficiently large constant γ, and input set Ui = P \ {pi} \ ⋃ pr∈R Si→r, takes Õ((cn/β + |Ui|)ℓ) time with probability ≥ 1− 1/nc for any constant c ≥ 1. Proof. By Claim 3.3 and a union bound, the maximum frequency, f , of any point in Ui is (c+1)n log n/β with probability ≥ 1 − 1/nc for any c ≥ 1. We then simply invoke Lemma 4.11, which states that VoteCover will take time O(f · min(ℓ, γOPTi log n) log n + |Ui|min(ℓ, γOPTi)) ≤ O((f log n + |Ui|)ℓ) ≤ O((cn log2(n)/β + |Ui|)ℓ). We first note that this bound already suffices to show that adding roughly (OPT∗/n) logn random neighbors to each point is enough to construct a graph with O(OPT∗ log n) edges in Õ(n2.25) time. Obtaining an Õ(n2) bound, however, requires more work. In particular, the running time bound in Claim 4.13 contains two terms. The first term, involving (n/β)min(ℓ,OPTi) can be bounded by n for each i if we add β ≈ OPTi random edges to each point, which would still lead to a near optimally sparse graph. While OPTi is not known in advance, it can be identified easily via an exponential search. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited The second term involving |Ui|min(ℓ,OPTi) is more difficult. In particular, suppose that ℓ is set high enough that VoteCover returns a cover of Ui (see Theorem 4.1). Then this term becomes Õ(|Ui|OPTi). Suppose we add β = OPT∗/n edges to each node, which is small enough that we can still construct a graph with O(OPT∗) edges. As shown in Corollary 3.4, after doing so, we will have ∑n i=1 |Ui| = Õ(n2/β). Suppose each |Ui| has roughly the same size of Õ(n/β). Then we would obtain a total running time of roughly: Õ ( n∑ i=1 |Ui|OPTi ) = Õ ( n∑ i=1 n β OPTi ) = n2 OPT∗ Õ ( n∑ i=1 OPTi ) = Õ(n2). Unfortunately, however, we do not expect U1, . . . , Un to be uniformly sized in general after random edges are added. Moreover, while a similar argument could be used to obtain Õ(n2) runtime if OPTi ≈ OPT∗/n for all i, again this will not hold in general. Different nodes could have widely varying optimal degrees. Our second preprocessing technique allows us to circumvent these issues by adding edges in a dependent way (via cliques), which allow us to reduce the number of uncovered points in a more targeted way across different set cover instances. Before getting into details, we first consider how the runtime of VoteCover is impacted by the addition of a single clique on a subset of nodes K (in addition to the random edges) Claim 4.14. If R1, . . . , Rn ⊂ P are uniform random samples of size β and K ⊆ P has size ≤ β, then running VoteCover on all pi ∈ K with size limit ℓ, sufficiently large constant γ, and input sets Ui = P \ {pi} \ ⋃ pr∈Ri Si→r \ ⋃ pk∈K Si→k takes Õ(cnℓ) total time with prob. ≥ 1− 1/nc+1 for any constant c ≥ 1. Proof. By Claim 4.13, running VoteCover on a single instance pi to cover P {pi}\ ⋃ pr∈Ri Si→r (and by extension any subset Ui thereof) takes O((cn log2(n)/β + |Ui|)ℓ) time with probability ≥ 1− 1/nc+2 for any c ≥ 1. Taking a union bound over all instances in K, this holds simultaneously across all ≤ β of those instances with probability ≥ 1− β/nc+2 ≥ 1− 1/nc+1. Recall from Claim 3.5 that ∑ pi∈K |Ui| ≤ n− β ≤ n. Therefore, the total time for running VoteCover on all instances in K is: O ∑ pi∈K ( cn log2 n β + |Ui| ) ℓ  = O ℓ ∑ pi∈K cn log2 n β + ℓ ∑ pi∈K |Ui|  = O ( cnℓ log2 n+ nℓ ) = Õ(cnℓ). 4.2.2 Full Algorithm With the above claims in place, we are equipped to augment Algorithm 4.2 with both random edges and arbitrary clique additions. The pseudocode of our final method that does so is given in Algorithm 4.3. We first give a high-level overview of the algorithm. The main loop, located on Line 2, iterates over doubling values of ℓ. This performs exponential search on OPTi for all instances Ii in parallel. We know that VoteCover with size limit ℓ will successfully return a cover for a point pi before ℓ exceeds OPTi lnn by more than a constant factor. Thus, we can supplement the cover with O(ℓ) other neighbors without violating our promised O(logn) approximation factor. Under this assumption, we add ℓ random edges and ℓ clique edges to each pi. We then efficiently filter out any points covered by these edges as described in Section 4.2.1, then run VoteCover with limit ℓ on each instance to cover the remaining points. For any instances that successfully return covers, we can build a full neighbor list by combining the random edges, the clique edges, and the cover from VoteCover. We subsequently remove these instances from contention, i.e., they will not be considered in future iterations. For any instances where VoteCover fails, we conclude that OPTi must be much larger than the current choice of ℓ and simply try again in the next iteration. 4.2.3 Correctness and Approximation Factor Here we will prove that Algorithm 4.3 always returns a navigable graph where each node’s out-degree is optimal to within an O(log n) factor. This proof follows easily from results proven earlier in this paper. Claim 4.15. Algorithm 4.3 returns a navigable graph. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Algorithm 4.3 SparsestNavigableGraph(P, d) Input: A point set P of size n, a distance function d, and constants c, ĉ > 0 that control failure probability. Output: A navigable graph on P representing by out-neighborhoods N1, . . . , Nn ⊆ P for each pi ∈ P . 1: Preprocess P as in Claim 3.2. Initialize Q← P . 2: for ℓ← 21, 22, . . . , 2⌈lgn⌉ do ▷ Exponential search on OPTi 3: Ui1 , Ui2 , . . . , Ui|Q| ← ∅ for each pij ∈ Q 4: Ri1 , Ri2 , . . . , Ri|Q| ← samples of P with size (c+ 3)ℓ for each pij ∈ Q ▷ Choose random edges 5: Arbitrarily partition Q into parts K1,K2, . . . ,K⌈|Q|/ℓ⌉ of size ≤ ℓ ▷ Choose arbitrary cliques 6: for K = K1,K2, . . . ,K⌈|Q|/ℓ⌉ do 7: for each pk ∈ P \K do ▷ Identify points not yet covered by random edges or clique edges 8: pi ← argminp∈K d(p, pk) 9: if pk ̸∈ Si→r for all pr ∈ Ri then 10: Ui ← Ui ∪ {pk} 11: for each pi ∈ K do ▷ Run VoteCover to cover all identified uncovered points 12: C ← VoteCover(P, pi, Ui, ℓ, 4ĉ+ 12) 13: if C ̸= Fail then 14: Q← Q \ {pi} 15: Ni ← Ri ∪K ∪ C \ {pi} 16: return Out-neighborhoods N1, . . . , Nn representing directed graph G. Proof. Since the algorithm keeps increasing ℓ to be ≥ n if the VoteCover call on Algorithm 4.3 fails, it is not hard to see that VoteCover eventually returns a solution for all pi (indeed, the algorithm succeeds with probability 1 if ℓ ≥ n). So, it suffices to show that the returned solution, Ri ∪K ∪C \ {pi} is a proper cover for set cover instance Ii. This is true because C is a cover for Ui and a point pk is only not added to Ui if it is already covered by an edge in K or in Ri. In particular, if pi ̸= argminp∈K d(p, pk) then it is covered by the edge from pi to argminp∈K d(p, pk) in the clique. Claim 4.16. With probability ≥ 1 − 1/nĉ, Algorithm 4.3 maintains the invariant that Q only contains points pi where OPTi > ℓ/((16ĉ+ 58) lnn). Proof. From Lemma 4.2, when run with ℓ ≥ (8ĉ + 29)OPTi lnn, VoteCover successfully returns a cover of size ≤ ℓ with probability ≥ 1 − 1/nĉ+1. Since ℓ doubles with each iteration of the main loop, it follows that for each i, there is an iteration prior to ℓ exceeding (16ĉ + 58)OPTi lnn where (8ĉ + 29)OPTi lnn ≤ ℓ < (16ĉ + 58)OPTi lnn. By a union bound, it holds simultaneously over all i that VoteCover returns a cover for Ii on that corresponding iteration with probability ≥ 1−1/nĉ, and possibly even earlier. Subsequently, the corresponding pi are removed from Q by Line 14. Thus, with this probability, all pi remaining in Q satisfy ℓ < (16ĉ+ 58)OPTi lnn, or equivalently OPTi > ℓ/((16ĉ+ 58) lnn). Corollary 4.17. With probability ≥ 1−1/nĉ, the graph returned by Algorithm 4.3 has O(cĉOPT∗ log n) edges, and each pi has O(cĉOPTi log n) out-neighbors. Proof. Each point’s set of neighbors is constructed as soon as VoteCover successfully returns some set C for that point. By Claim 4.16, with probability≥ 1−1/nĉ, this happens for pi when ℓ ≤ (16ĉ+58)OPTi lnn. On the corresponding iteration of the main loop, Ri has size (c + 3)ℓ, and all partitions K have size ≤ ℓ. Therefore, each pi receives at most |R ∪K ∪ C| ≤ (c+ 5)ℓ ≤ (c+ 5)(16ĉ+ 58)OPTi lnn = O(cĉOPTi log n) out-neighbors. By extension, the total number of edges in the graph is O(cĉOPT∗ log n). 4.2.4 Running Time To finish the proof of Theorem 1.2, we analyze the running time for Algorithm 4.3. Lemma 4.18. Each iteration of the main loop takes Õ(cn2) time with probability ≥ 1− 1/nc+1. Proof. Lines 6-15 constitute the nontrivial running time of a given iteration of the main loop. For each clique K, these lines perform two main steps. Copyright © 2026 bySIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited
Lines 7–10: The algorithm initializes the set cover instances within K. This involves filtering out all points in these instances which are already covered either by clique edges or random edges.
Lines 11–15: The algorithm runs VoteCover on each set cover instance in K to cover all remaining points that Lines 7–10 identified as not yet covered. Initializing set cover instances. Line 8 is done using a single scan over K, computing the distance between pk and each member of K to determine the minimum among them. This takes O(|K|) = O(ℓ) time per execution of Line 8. Likewise, Line 9 performs a scan over Ri, which takes O(|Ri|) = O(ℓ) time. Lines 8 and 9 are run n− |K| = O(n) times per clique, of which there are at most O(n/ℓ). Thus, the total amount of work across all cliques is O(ℓ) ·O(n) ·O(n/ℓ) = O(n2). Running VoteCover. By Claim 4.14, the calls to VoteCover for a given clique K run in com-bined time O(cnℓ) with probability ≥ 1 − 1/nc+2. Since there are at most ⌈n/ℓ⌉ cliques, this takes O(cnℓ log2 n) ·O(n/ℓ) = O(cn2 log2 n) time in total with probability ≥ 1− 1/nc+1 (by a union bound). Corollary 4.19. Algorithm 4.3 runs in Õ(cn2) time with probability ≥ 1− 1/nc. Proof. This follows easily from the previous lemma. The main loop runs for at most ⌈log2 n⌉ iterations, each taking O(n2 log2 n) time with probability ≤ 1−1/nc+1. Taking a union bound over ≤ ⌈log2 n⌉ iterations, this holds for all levels simultaneously with probability ≥ 1 − ⌈log2 n⌉/nc+1 > 1 − 1/nc, in which case the algorithm takes a total of O(n2 log3 n) time. Theorem 1.2 follows as an immediate consequence of Corollary 4.17 and Corollary 4.19. 5 Hardness In this section we establish hardness results to show that the O(log n) approximation achieved by our algorithm for constructing minimum sparsity navigable graphs is essentially tight for any polynomial-time algorithm, even when the input point set is in low-dimensional Euclidean space (e.g., O(log(n)) dimensions). We start with preliminary terminology, definitions and lemmas we will use in proving the results. 5.1 Preliminaries We first introduce some additional terminology related to the “local” definition of navigability in Definition 1.1. Definition 5.1. Given point set P and distance metric d : P × P → R+, and given a point p ∈ P and a set P ′ ⊆ P , P ′ ⊆ P is a navigable neighborhood for p if and only if for every p′′ ∈ P where p ̸= p′′, there exists some p′ ∈ P ′ such that d(p′, p′′) < d(p, p′′). Definition 5.2. Given point set P and distance metric d : P × P → R+, and given a point p ∈ P and a set P ′ ⊆ P , we say that P ′ ⊆ P is a minimum navigable neighborhood for p if and only if for every navigable neighborhood P ′′ ⊆ P we have |P ′| ≤ |P ′′|. We say that P ′ ⊆ P is a c-approximate minimum navigable neighborhood for p if and only if for every navigable neighborhood P ′′ ⊆ P we have |P ′| ≤ c · |P ′′|. A graph is navigable if and only if the out-neighborhood of every node p is a navigable neighborhood for p. Further, a graph is a minimum navigable graph if and only if the out-neighborhood of every p is the minimum navigable neighborhood. Note however, that if we have a c-approximate minimum navigable graph in terms of total number of edges, it does not mean that any particular neighborhood is c-approximate minimum. As such, proving hardness results nearly minimizing total degree (equivalently, average degree) is more challenging than proving such results for nearly minimizing the neighborhood size of any particular p. 5.1.1 Defining Covering Point Sets Our hardness results will be based on the hardness of solving set cover, either exactly and approximately. As such, we start by describing a transformation from a set cover instance to a point set, with the property that navigable neighborhoods for the point set can be tranlated into a solution to the original set cover instance. We will instantiate this transformation in different settings to obtain various hardness results. Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Definition 5.3. Given a set cover instance (U = [n],F = {S1, ..., Sm}), a point set P is called a representative point set for (U,F) if P(U,F) = {pi : i ∈ [n]} ∪ {pSj : Sj ∈ F} ⊆ P , meaning P contains a point for every element, which we term element points, and every set, which we term set points. Further, P contains a distinguished point, c ∈ P \ P(U,F) which we term the centroid. Points in (P \ P(U,F)) \ {c}, i.e the remaining points, are called solution points. We denote the set of solution points by PSC . Definition 5.4. Given a set cover instance (U = [n],F = {S1, ..., Sm}), a point set P is called a covering point set for (U,F) with respect to distance metric d on P , if it is a representative point set, and the following properties hold:
∀p ∈ PSC , p ′ ∈ P {p, c}, d(p, c) < d(p, p′). I.e., c is the unique nearest neighbor for any solution point.
∀i, j ∈ [m], p ∈ PSC , d(pSi , pSj ) < d(pSi , p). I.e., any set point is closer to any other set point than to a solution point.
∀p ∈ PSC , p ′ ∈ P, i ∈ [n], d(p′, pi) < d(p, pi) if and only if p′ = pi or p′ = pSj where i ∈ Sj. I.e., moving from a solution point to another point gets us closer to an element point pi if and only if the other point is equal to pi or is the point corresponding to a set that contains i.
∀p, p′ ∈ PSC , p ′′ ∈ (P \ PSC) \ {c}, d(p, p′) < d(p, p′′). I.e., any solution point is closer to any other solution point than it is to any element or set point. 5.1.2 Properties of Covering Point Sets Observation 5.5. In a covering point set, an element point or a set point has a minimum navigable neighborhood which size is at most m+ n+ 1. Proof. Let p be an element or a set point, and consider N = (P \PSC){p} as a candidate neighborhood for p. All set and element points are in N and so is c. Further, according to property (1) of covering point sets, c is closer than p to all points in PSC , thus N is a navigable neighborhood with size at most n+m+1. Observation 5.6. Given a covering point set for a set cover instance (U,F), a solution point p ∈ PSC , and a navigable neighborhood N ⊆ P for p, we can efficiently (in time polynomial in n,m) compute a set cover for (U,F) with size at most |N | − 1. Proof. Define the set N̂ = (N \ PSC) \ {c}. According to property (4) of covering point sets, and since N is a navigable neighborhood for p, N̂ is a strict subset of N . In particular, we would only have N̂ = N if N only contains set or element points. This cannot be the case by property (4) of covering point sets, as no set or element points are closer to p′i ∈ PSC than p is. Further, according to property (3) of covering point sets, for every i ∈ [n], either pi ∈ N or pSj ∈ N where i ∈ Sj . Thus, if we swap every element point in N̂ with a set point of an arbitrary set that contains the element, we get a collection of at most |N | − 1 set points, whose corresponding sets cover all elements in U = [n]. Lemma 5.7. In a covering point set, the size of a minimum navigable neighborhood for a solution point is exactly OPT+ 1 where OPT is the size of the minimum set cover for (U,F). Proof. Let p ∈ PSC , if F ′ ⊆ F is a set cover, then N = {pSj : Sj ∈ F ′} ∪ {c} i,e all points representing sets in F ′ and the centroid, are a navigable neighborhood for p. To see why this is true - note that c is in N , and any set in F ′ (which is also in N) gets us closer to all other sets according to property (2) of covering point sets. Further, for every i ∈ [n] there exists Sj ∈ F ′ ⊆ N such that i ∈ Sj and thus according to property (3) of covering point sets d(pSj , pi) < d(p, pi) - i.e we can get closer to all element points. This implies that the size of the minimum navigable neighborhood is at most OPT + 1. In the other direction -note that if N is a navigable neighborhood for p, then according to Theorem 5.6, there exists a set cover of size |N | − 1, and thus |N | ≥ OPT+ 1. As this lemma shows, the main strength of covering point sets, is that the problem of finding a navigable neighborhood for a solution point corresponds to computing a set cover for the original instance. A crucial Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited part, demonstrated in the previous observations, is that the size of minimum navigable neighborhoods for set and element points does not scale with the number of solution points (due to the centroid), so by including moderately many solution points in the construction we can claim that in a navigable graph with approximate-minimum average degree, some solution point has a navigable neighborhood that reasonably approximates the minimum. This will be central to the way we use these constructions. Corollary 5.8. The total number of edges in a minimum navigable graph for a covering point set is at most (n+m)(n+m+ 1) +m+ n+ |PSC |+ (OPT+ 1) · |PSC |. Lemma 5.9. Let (U,F) be a set cover instance, and let P be a covering point set. Then, if |PSC | ≥ (m + n)3, and G is a navigable graph for P which average degree is r-approximate minimum, then we can use G to efficiently (in time polynomial and m,n), compute a ( r + o(1) + Θ ( r OPT )) -approximate minimum set cover for (U,F). Proof. First we show there must exist some solution point in P which degree in G is at most( r + o(1) + 1 OPT ) ·(OPT+1). Indeed let λ > 0 such that all solution points have degree at least λ ·(OPT+1). This implies that the sum of degrees in G is at least λ(m + n)3 · (OPT + 1). On the other hand, we know from Corollary 5.8, and since G’s average degree is r-approximate minimum that the sum of degrees in G is at most: r · ( (n+m) (n+m+ 1) +m+ n+ (m+ n) 3 + (OPT+ 1) · (m+ n) 3 ) So: λ (m+ n) 3 · (OPT+ 1) ≤ r · ( (n+m) (n+m+ 1) +m+ n+ (m+ n) 3 + (OPT+ 1) · (m+ n) 3 ) Thus: λ ≤ r · ( (n+m) (n+m+ 1) +m+ n+ (m+ n) 3 + (OPT+ 1) · (m+ n) 3 ) (m+ n) 3 · (OPT+ 1) ≤ r + Θ ( (m+ n) 2 ) (m+ n) 3 · (OPT+ 1)
1 OPT+ 1 ≤ r + o (1) + 1 OPT So, there exists a solution point with neighborhood in G which size is at most: (5.1) ( r + o(1) + 1 OPT ) · (OPT+ 1) = OPT · ( r + o(1) + Θ ( r OPT )) Now, recall Theorem 5.6, which implies that for each solution point, we can efficiently compute a set cover which size is at most the size of its neighborhood. Thus, we can use the solution point that has minimal degree, which as we’ve established is at most OPT · ( r + o(1) + Θ ( r OPT )) , to compute a set cover of this size, i.e a ( r + o(1) + Θ ( r OPT )) -approximate minimum set cover. 5.2 Hardness for General Distance Metrics The first step towards establishing hardness of approximation is, given a set cover instance (U,F), to show how to actually construct a covering point set for this instance. This is quite straightforward if we are allowed to use a general metric. Specifically, we define a mapping φ(U,F) = (P, d) that maps the set cover instance to a representative point set P and distance metric d which are specified as follows: P has (n+m)2 solution points, d is a symmetric function, such that d(p, p′) = 0 ⇐⇒ p = p′. We specify d in table form (see Figure 5.1). Note that d satisfies triangle inequality since the values it assigns to distances between distinct points are in {1, 1.5, 2}. It is straightforward to verify that (P, d) is a covering point set: For property (1) - Observe that the distance of c from a solution point is smaller than all other distances in the solution points column. For property (2) observe that the distance between set points is 1, while the distance between a set point Copyright © 2026 by SIAM Unauthorized reproduction of this article is prohibited Element points Set points Solution points c Element points 2 d ( pi, pSj ) = { 1 i ∈ Sj 2 else 2 2 Set points ” 1 2 2 Solution points ” ” 1.5 1 c ” ” ” 0 Figure 5.1: Specification of distance metric d on different types of points in P . and a solution point is 2. For property (3), observe that the only point that has a distance smaller than 1.5 from an element, is the element itself, and any set that contains it. For property (4) observe that the distance between solution points is 1.5, while their distance from set and element points is 2. Theorem 5.10. For any ε > 0, unless P = NP , there does not exist a polynomial-time algorithm that, given a set P of n points and a distance metric d : P × P → R+, constructs a navigable graph with average degree ≤ ( 13 · ln(n)− ε) · OPT, where OPT is the minimum average degree of any navigable graph on (P, d). Proof. Let ε > 0, and assume the existence of such an algorithm. Set 0 < ε′ = ε 2 < ε, and observe section 6.2 of [18]: The instances their reduction constructs, that are NP -hard to log(n)− ε′ approximate, have N = nD+1 1 elements and M = n2 1 sets where D > 1 is inversely proportional to ε′. So, M ≤ N . Further, the size of the optimal solution for such an instance satisfies OPT ∈ Ω (n1) = Ω ( N 1 D+1 ) . We show that the existence of the hypothesized algorithm implies an efficient algorithm to construct a ln(N)− ε′-approximate minimum set cover for such instances, implying P = NP . Consider such an instance (U,F) with N elements, M sets and an optimal solution of size OPT which all behave as we’ve stated above and construct a covering point set P and distance metric d as described in the preceding discussion - note that this point set has N ′ = M + N + 1 + (M + N)2 points. We can run the hypothesized algorithm on (P, d) and retrieve a navigable graph which average degree is r-approximate minimum where r = 1 3 · ln(N ′) − ε. According to Lemma 5.9, we can then use this graph to efficiently compute a set cover for (U,F) which size is at most OPT ( r + o(1) + Θ ( r OPT )) = OPT(r + o(1)) = OPT ( 1 3 · ln(N ′)− ε+ o(1) ) ≤ OPT ( 1 3 · ln(N ′)− ε′ ) OPT ( 1 3 · ln(M +N + 1 + (M +N)2)− ε′ ) ≤ OPT ( 1 3 · ln(N3)− ε′ ) = OPT(ln(N)− ε′) So, we have a ln(N) − ε′ approximation in polynomial time for NP -hard-to-approximate instances, which implies P = NP . 5.3 Hardness for Euclidean Metrics To show hardness of approximation for Euclidean metrics, we demonstrate how a covering point set can be constructed in Euclidean space. From there, the proof for hardness of approximation is identical to Theorem 5.10 and can be derived as a corollary.
